href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup> Scathing criticism for this action from Palestine [Chief Justice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chief_Justice "Chief Justice") Sir [Michael McDonnell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_McDonnell "Michael McDonnell") was not well received by the administration and the judge was soon removed from the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999399_119-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999399-119">[113]</a></sup> Villages were also frequently punished by fines and confiscation of livestock.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-12"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup> The British even used sea mines from the battleship [HMS _Malaya_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HMS_Malaya "HMS Malaya") to destroy houses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-13"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup>

In addition to actions against property, a large amount of brutality by the British forces occurred, including beatings, torture and extrajudicial killings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-14"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup> A surprisingly large number of prisoners were "shot while trying to escape".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-15"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup> Several incidents involved serious atrocities, such as massacres at [al-Bassa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Bassa "Al-Bassa") and [Halhul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halhul "Halhul").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-16"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup> Desmond Woods, an officer of the [Royal Ulster Rifles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Ulster_Rifles "Royal Ulster Rifles"), described the massacre at al-Bassa:

> Now I will never forget this incident ... We were at al-Malikiyya, the other frontier base and word came through about 6 o'clock in the morning that one of our patrols had been blown up and Millie Law \[the dead officer\] had been killed. Now Gerald Whitfeld \[Lieutenant-Colonel G.H.P. Whitfeld, the battalion commander\] had told these mukhtars that if any of this sort of thing happened he would take punitive measures against the nearest village to the scene of the mine. Well the nearest village to the scene of the mine was a place called al-Bassa and our Company C were ordered to take part in punitive measures. And I will never forget arriving at al-Bassa and seeing the [Rolls-Royce armoured cars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Armoured_Car "Rolls-Royce Armoured Car") of the [11th Hussars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/11th_Hussars "11th Hussars") peppering Bassa with machine gun fire and this went on for about 20 minutes and then we went in and I remembered we had lighted braziers and we set the houses on fire and we burnt the village to the ground ... Monty had him \[the battalion commander\] up and he asked him all about it and Gerald Whitfeld explained to him. He said "Sir, I have warned the [mukhtars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mukhtar "Mukhtar") in these villages that if this happened to any of my officers or men, I would take punitive measures against them and I did this and I would've lost control of the frontier if I hadn't." [Monty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernard_Law_Montgomery,_1st_Viscount_Montgomery_of_Alamein "Bernard Law Montgomery, 1st Viscount Montgomery of Alamein") said "All right but just go a wee bit easier in the future."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-17"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup>

As well as destroying the village the RUR and men from the [Royal Engineers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Engineers "Royal Engineers") collected around fifty men from al-Bassa and blew some of them up with explosion under a bus. Harry Arrigonie, a policeman who was present said that about twenty men were put onto a bus; those who tried to escape were shot and then the driver of the bus was forced to drive over a powerful land mine buried by the soldiers which completely destroyed the bus, scattering the mutilated bodies of the prisoners everywhere. The other villagers were then forced to bury the bodies in a pit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-18"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup>

Despite these measures Lieutenant-General Haining, the General Officer Commanding, reported secretly to the Cabinet on 1 December 1938 that "practically every village in the country harbours and supports the rebels and will assist in concealing their identity from the Government Forces."<sup id="cite_ref-haining_120-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-haining-120">[114]</a></sup> Haining reported the method for searching villages:

> A cordon round the area to be searched is first established either by troops or aircraft and the inhabitants are warned that anybody trying to break through the cordon is likely to be shot. As literally hundreds of villages have been searched, in some cases more than once, during the past six months this procedure is well-known and it can be safely assumed that cordon-breakers have good reasons for wishing to avoid the troops. A number of such cordon-breakers have been shot during searches and it is probable that such cases form the basis of the propaganda that Arab prisoners are shot in cold blood and reported as "killed while trying to escape". After the cordon is established the troops enter the village and all male inhabitants are collected for identification and interrogation.<sup id="cite_ref-haining_120-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-haining-120">[114]</a></sup>

The report was issued in response to growing concern at the severity of the military measures amongst the general public in Great Britain, among members of the British Government, and among governments in countries neighbouring Palestine.<sup id="cite_ref-haining_120-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-haining-120">[114]</a></sup>

In addition to actions against villages the British Army also conducted punitive actions in the cities. In [Nablus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nablus "Nablus") in August 1938 almost 5,000 men were held in a cage for two days and interrogated one after another.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999424_121-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999424-121">[115]</a></sup> During their detention the city was searched and then each of the detainees was marked with a rubber stamp on his release.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999424_121-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999424-121">[115]</a></sup> At one point a night curfew was imposed on most of the cities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999424_121-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999424-121">[115]</a></sup>

It was common British army practice to make local Arabs ride with military convoys to prevent mine attacks and sniping incidents: soldiers would tie the hostages to the bonnets of lorries, or put them on small flatbeds on the front of trains.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-19"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup> The army told the hostages that any of them who tried to run away would be shot. On the lorries, some soldiers would brake hard at the end of a journey and then casually drive over the hostage, killing or maiming him, as Arthur Lane, a [Manchester Regiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manchester_Regiment "Manchester Regiment") private recalled:

>  ... when you'd finished your duty you would come away nothing had happened no bombs or anything and the driver would switch his wheel back and to make the truck waver and the poor wog on the front would roll off into the deck. Well if he was lucky he'd get away with a broken leg but if he was unlucky the truck behind coming up behind would hit him. But nobody bothered to pick up the bits they were left. You know we were there we were the masters we were the bosses and whatever we did was right ... Well you know you don't want him any more. He's fulfilled his job. And that's when Bill Usher \[the commanding officer\] said that it had to stop because before long they'd be running out of bloody rebels to sit on the bonnet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-20"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup>

British troops also left Arab wounded on the battlefield to die and maltreated Arab fighters taken in battle, so much so that the rebels tried to remove their wounded or dead from the field of battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-21"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup> Sometimes, soldiers would occupy villages, expel all of the inhabitants and remain for months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999425_122-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999425-122">[116]</a></sup> The Army even burned the bodies of "terrorists" to prevent their funerals becoming the focus of protests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999425_122-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999425-122">[116]</a></sup>

Nevertheless, it has been argued that British behaviour overall was good compared to most other examples where a foreign army suppressed a popular insurgency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-22"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup>

### Tegart forts

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Latrun-Police-Building.jpg/220px-Latrun-Police-Building.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Latrun-Police-Building.jpg)

A surviving police [Tegart fort](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tegart_fort "Tegart fort") at [Latrun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latrun "Latrun") devised by Sir [Charles Tegart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Tegart "Charles Tegart"), who also introduced border fences and [Arab Investigation Centres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Investigation_Centres "Arab Investigation Centres").

Sir [Charles Tegart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Tegart "Charles Tegart") was a senior police officer brought into Palestine from the colonial force of [British India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidencies_and_provinces_of_British_India "Presidencies and provinces of British India")<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-23"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup> on 21 October 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003235–236_123-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003235%E2%80%93236-123">[117]</a></sup> Tegart and his deputy [David Petrie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Petrie "David Petrie") (later head of [MI5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MI5 "MI5")) advised a greater emphasis on foreign intelligence gathering and closure of Palestine's borders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008254_124-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008254-124">[118]</a></sup> Like many of those enrolled in the Palestinian gendarmerie, Tegart had served in Great Britain's repression of the [Irish War of Independence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence "Irish War of Independence"), and the security proposals he introduced exceeded measures adopted down to this time elsewhere in the British Empire. 70 fortresses were erected throughout the country at strategic choke points and near Palestinian villages which, if assessed as "bad", were subjected to [collective punishment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collective_punishment "Collective punishment").<sup id="cite_ref-125"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-125">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoffman201573_126-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoffman201573-126">[120]</a></sup> Accordingly, from 1938 [Gilbert Mackereth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gilbert_Mackereth "Gilbert Mackereth"), the British Consul in Damascus, corresponded with Syrian and Transjordan authorities regarding border control and security to counteract arms smuggling and "terrorist" infiltration and produced a report for Tegart on the activities of the Palestine Defence Committee in Damascus.<sup id="cite_ref-127"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-127">[121]</a></sup> Tegart recommended the construction of a frontier road with a barbed wire fence, which became known as Tegart's wall, along the borders with [Lebanon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebanon "Lebanon") and [Syria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syria "Syria") to help prevent the flow of insurgents, goods and weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003235–236_123-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003235%E2%80%93236-123">[117]</a></sup> Tegart encouraged close co-operation with the Jewish Agency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999428_128-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999428-128">[122]</a></sup> It was built by the [Histadrut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Histadrut "Histadrut") construction company [Solel Boneh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solel_Boneh "Solel Boneh").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999428_128-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999428-128">[122]</a></sup> The total cost was £2 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999428_128-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999428-128">[122]</a></sup> The Army forced the fellahin to work on the roads without pay.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999150_129-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999150-129">[123]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Almog_IL15_Pillboxgoren.jpg/220px-Almog_IL15_Pillboxgoren.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Almog_IL15_Pillboxgoren.jpg)

Pillbox built along the route of [Tegart's wall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tegart%27s_wall "Tegart's wall"), still standing today near [Goren](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goren "Goren") industrial zone, northern [Israel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel "Israel")

Tegart introduced [Arab Investigation Centres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Investigation_Centres "Arab Investigation Centres") where prisoners were subjected to beatings, [foot whipping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foot_whipping "Foot whipping"), electric shocks, [denailing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Denailing "Denailing") and what is now known as "[waterboarding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Waterboarding "Waterboarding")".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-24"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup> Tegart also imported [Doberman Pinschers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doberman_Pinscher "Doberman Pinscher") from South Africa and set up a special centre in Jerusalem to train interrogators in torture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999416_130-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999416-130">[124]</a></sup>

### Role of the Royal Air Force

The [Royal Air Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Air_Force "Royal Air Force") developed [close air support](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Close_air_support "Close air support") into its then most refined form during the Arab Revolt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074–76_131-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074%E2%80%9376-131">[125]</a></sup> Air patrols had been found effective in keeping convoys and trains free from attack, but this did not help to expose insurgents to battle conditions likely to cause their defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074–76_131-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074%E2%80%9376-131">[125]</a></sup> From the middle of June 1936 wireless vehicles accompanied all convoys and patrols.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074–76_131-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074%E2%80%9376-131">[125]</a></sup> During rebel attacks these vehicles could issue emergency "XX calls" (XX with a coded location), which were given priority over all other radio traffic, to summon aerial reinforcements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074–76_131-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074%E2%80%9376-131">[125]</a></sup> Bombers, which were usually airborne within five minutes, could then either attack insurgents directly or "fix" their position for infantry troops. Forty-seven such XX calls were issued during the revolt, causing heavy losses to the rebels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074–76_131-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074%E2%80%9376-131">[125]</a></sup> In the June 1936 [Battle of Nur Shams](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Nur_Shams "Battle of Nur Shams") British planes attacked Arab irregulars with machine gun fire.

This use of air power was so successful that the British were able to reduce the regular garrison.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074–76_131-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOmissi199074%E2%80%9376-131">[125]</a></sup>

In 1936 an Air Staff Officer in [Middle East Command](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_East_Command "Middle East Command") based in the [Kingdom of Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Egypt "Kingdom of Egypt"), [Arthur Harris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthur_Harris "Arthur Harris"), known as an advocate of "air policing",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERotter200851_132-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTERotter200851-132">[126]</a></sup> commented on the revolt saying that "one 250 lb. or 500 lb. bomb in each village that speaks out of turn" would satisfactorily solve the problem.<sup id="cite_ref-133"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-133">[127]</a></sup> In 1937 Harris was promoted to [Air Commodore](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air_Commodore "Air Commodore") and in 1938 he was posted to [Palestine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine "Mandatory Palestine") and [Trans-Jordan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emirate_of_Transjordan "Emirate of Transjordan") as [Air Officer Commanding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air_Officer_Commanding "Air Officer Commanding") the RAF contingent in the region until September 1939. "Limited" bombing attacks on Arab villages were carried out by the RAF,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOmissi1990158_134-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOmissi1990158-134">[128]</a></sup> although at times this involved razing whole villages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBen-Ami200511_135-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBen-Ami200511-135">[129]</a></sup> Harris described the system by which recalcitrant villages were kept under control by [aerial bombardment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aerial_bombing_of_cities "Aerial bombing of cities") as "Air-Pin".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris199830_136-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris199830-136">[130]</a></sup>

Aircraft of the RAF were also used to drop propaganda leaflets over Palestinian towns and villages telling the fellahin that they were the main sufferers of the rebellion and threatening an increase in taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999150_129-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999150-129">[123]</a></sup>

Low flying RAF squadrons were able to produce detailed intelligence on the location of [road blocks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roadblock_(barrier) "Roadblock (barrier)"), sabotaged bridges, railways and pipelines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008246_137-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008246-137">[131]</a></sup> RAF aerial photographs were also used to build up a detailed map of Arab population distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008246_137-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008246-137">[131]</a></sup>

Although the British Army was responsible for setting up the Arab counter-insurgent forces (known as the peace bands) and supplying them with arms and money these were operated by [RAF Intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RAF_Intelligence "RAF Intelligence"), commanded by [Patrick Domville](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Patrick_Domville&action=edit&redlink=1 "Patrick Domville (page does not exist)").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlackMorris199116_138-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlackMorris199116-138">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarouvi199933_139-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarouvi199933-139">[133]</a></sup>

At the beginning of the revolt RAF assets in the region comprised a bomber flight at RAF Ramleh, an [RAF armoured car flight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RAF_Regiment "RAF Regiment") at Ramleh, fourteen bomber squadrons at RAF Amman, and a RAF armoured car company at [Ma'an](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ma%27an "Ma'an").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillman199822_5-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillman199822-5">[5]</a></sup>

### Role of the Royal Navy

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/HMS_Malaya.jpg/220px-HMS_Malaya.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:HMS_Malaya.jpg)

The [Royal Navy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Navy "Royal Navy") used naval mines from [HMS _Malaya_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HMS_Malaya "HMS Malaya") to destroy Palestinian houses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-25"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup>

At the beginning of the Revolt crew from the Haifa Naval Force's two [cruisers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cruiser "Cruiser") were used to carry out tasks ashore, manning two [howitzers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Howitzer "Howitzer") and naval lorries equipped with [QF 2 pounder naval guns](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/QF_2_pounder_naval_gun "QF 2 pounder naval gun") and searchlights used to disperse Arab snipers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup> From the end of June two [destroyers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Destroyer "Destroyer") were used to patrol the coast of Palestine in a bid to prevent [gun running](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gun_running "Gun running").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup> These searched as many as 150 vessels per week and were an effective preventive measure.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup> At the request of the Army additional naval platoons landed in July to help protect Haifa and Jewish settlements in the surrounding countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup> The Navy also relieved the Army of duties in Haifa by using nine naval platoons to form the _Haifa Town Force_ and in August three naval platoons were landed to support the police.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup>

Following publication of the Peel Commission's report in July 1937 [HMS _Repulse_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HMS_Repulse_(1916) "HMS Repulse (1916)") sailed to Haifa where landing parties were put ashore to maintain calm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup> Various other naval vessels continued with this role until the end of the revolt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-6"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup>

Following the Irgun's detonation of a large bomb in a market in Haifa on 6 July 1938 the High Commissioner signalled the Commander-in-Chief of the [Mediterranean Fleet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediterranean_Fleet "Mediterranean Fleet"), Admiral Sir [Dudley Pound](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dudley_Pound "Dudley Pound"), requesting the assistance of naval vessels capable of providing landing parties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-7"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup> Pound dispatched HMS _Repulse_ and diverted [HMS _Emerald_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HMS_Emerald_(D66) "HMS Emerald (D66)") to Haifa, which arrived the same day and landed five platoons, one to each police district.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-8"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup> HMS _Repulse_ relieved HMS _Emerald_ the following day and after another bomb was detonated on 10 July five platoons from the ship, made up of sailors and [Royal Marines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Marines "Royal Marines"), dispersed mobs and patrolled the city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-9"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup>

On 11 July provision of three platoons from _Repulse_ released men of the [West Kent Regiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Queen%27s_Own_Royal_West_Kent_Regiment "The Queen's Own Royal West Kent Regiment") for a punitive mission against Arabs who had attacked a Jewish colony near Haifa.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-10"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup> By 17 July the _Repulse_ established a Company Headquarters where seamen and Royal Marines manned a 3.7-inch howitzer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-11"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup> Sailors, Royal Marines, and men of the [Suffolk Regiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suffolk_Regiment "Suffolk Regiment"), who had embarked on the _Repulse_, accompanied foot patrols of the [Palestine Police Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestine_Police_Force "Palestine Police Force").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-12"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup>

The _Repulse_, [HMS _Hood_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HMS_Hood "HMS Hood") and [HMS _Warspite_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HMS_Warspite_(03) "HMS Warspite (03)") provided howitzer crews which were sent ashore to combat gun running near the border with [Lebanon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebanon "Lebanon").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-13"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup> Detained Arabs were used to build [emplacements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fortification "Fortification") and the howitzers were moved quickly between these positions by day and night to confuse bandits as to the likely direction of fire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-14"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup> Periodically, the guns were used to fire warning rounds close to the vicinity of villages believed to have rebel sympathies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart20027–10_140-15"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart20027%E2%80%9310-140">[134]</a></sup>

#### Strategic importance of Haifa

Britain had completed the modern deep-sea port in Haifa in 1933 and finished laying a pipeline from the Iraqi [oilfields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oilfield "Oilfield") to Haifa in 1935,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrämer2008293_141-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKr%C3%A4mer2008293-141">[135]</a></sup> shortly before the outbreak of the revolt. A refinery for processing oil from the pipeline was completed by [Consolidated Refineries Ltd](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consolidated_Refineries_Ltd "Consolidated Refineries Ltd"), a company jointly owned by [British Petroleum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Petroleum "British Petroleum") and [Royal Dutch Shell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell "Royal Dutch Shell"), in December 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrierBamberg1994165_142-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrierBamberg1994165-142">[136]</a></sup>

These facilities enhanced the strategic importance of Palestine and of Haifa in particular in Britain's control of the eastern [Mediterranean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediterranean "Mediterranean").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrämer2008293_141-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKr%C3%A4mer2008293-141">[135]</a></sup> The threat to British control of the region posed by the [Italian invasion of Abyssinia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Italo-Abyssinian_War "Second Italo-Abyssinian War") in October 1935 and the deteriorating situation in Europe toward the end of the 1930s probably made British policy makers more willing to make concessions to Arab governments on the Palestine issue following the furore over the recommendations of the Peel Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrämer2008293_141-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKr%C3%A4mer2008293-141">[135]</a></sup>

### Role of the British intelligence services

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Reuven_Shiloah.jpg/220px-Reuven_Shiloah.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Reuven_Shiloah.jpg)

[Reuven Zaslany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reuven_Shiloah "Reuven Shiloah") (Shiloah), later first director of [Mossad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mossad "Mossad"), worked closely with British intelligence during the Arab Revolt.

The Arab Revolt was the last major test of Britain's security services in the Middle East before World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244–246_143-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244%E2%80%93246-143">[137]</a></sup> The development and deployment of intelligence-led counterinsurgency strategies was integral to the restoration of British [imperial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Empire "British Empire") control in Palestine as the revolt had demonstrated to the British authorities how a [popular rebellion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolution "Revolution") could undermine intelligence gathering operations and thereby impair their ability to predict and respond to inter-communal disorder.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244–246_143-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244%E2%80%93246-143">[137]</a></sup> The rebellion had brought together urban nationalism and peasant economic grievances arising from rural poverty and landlessness, which was blamed on British misrule.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244–246_143-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244%E2%80%93246-143">[137]</a></sup> Accordingly, the Palestinian revolt targeted the political and economic apparatus of the British [colonial state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colony "Colony"), including the communications network, pipelines, police stations, army outposts and British personnel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244–246_143-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244%E2%80%93246-143">[137]</a></sup> It was this aspect of the revolt, rather than attacks on Jews or violence between rivals for leadership of the national movement, that most concerned the high commissioner.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244–246_143-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244%E2%80%93246-143">[137]</a></sup> The mandate authorities were further disturbed by the unity of purpose displayed during the six-month [general strike](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestinian_general_strike "Palestinian general strike") and by the resurgence of [pan-Arab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-Arabism "Pan-Arabism") nationalism as evidenced by the rise of the [Istiqlal Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Independence_Party_(Palestine) "Independence Party (Palestine)").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244–246_143-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244%E2%80%93246-143">[137]</a></sup>

In response to these challenges the British army command ("I" Branch) and battalion headquarters across Palestine issued a daily intelligence bulletin every afternoon detailing political developments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244–246_143-6"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244%E2%80%93246-143">[137]</a></sup> Special Service Officers (SSOs) assigned to intelligence gathering reported directly to their local command headquarters and their cars were equipped with wireless transmitters so that high grade intelligence could be reported directly to "I" Branch immediately.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244–246_143-7"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244%E2%80%93246-143">[137]</a></sup> These sources of intelligence gradually became more important than those of the [C.I.D.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Criminal_Investigation_Department "Criminal Investigation Department") in Palestine, which had been dependent on Arab informers, and which were no longer reliable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244–246_143-8"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2008244%E2%80%93246-143">[137]</a></sup>

In September 1937, the Jewish Agency appointed [Reuven Zaslany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reuven_Shiloah "Reuven Shiloah") liaison officer for intelligence and security affairs between the Political Department of the Jewish Agency and the intelligence arms of the Royal Air Force and the C.I.D.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarouvi199932–34_144-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarouvi199932%E2%80%9334-144">[138]</a></sup> Zaslany sifted through intelligence collected by Jewish-controlled field operatives and forwarded it to the British military.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarouvi199932–34_144-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarouvi199932%E2%80%9334-144">[138]</a></sup> He was a frequent visitor at the headquarters of British intelligence and the army, the police and C.I.D. and he also travelled to [Damascus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Damascus "Damascus") to liaise with the Arab opposition's peace bands and with the [British Consul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consul_(representative) "Consul (representative)") in Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarouvi199932–34_144-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarouvi199932%E2%80%9334-144">[138]</a></sup> Colonel [Frederick Kisch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frederick_Kisch "Frederick Kisch"), a British army officer and Zionist leader, was appointed chief liaison officer between the British army and the Jewish Agency Executive with Zaslany as his deputy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarouvi199932–34_144-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarouvi199932%E2%80%9334-144">[138]</a></sup> Zaslany also worked as interpreter for Patrick Domville, head of [RAF Intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RAF_Intelligence "RAF Intelligence") in Palestine (who was described by Haganah leader Dov Hos as the "best Zionist informer on the English"), until the latter was posted to Iraq in 1938, and through him became acquainted with many of the British intelligence officers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriling2005279_145-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriling2005279-145">[139]</a></sup>

In 1937 the Jewish Agency's intelligence groups were responsible for bugging the Peel Commission hearings in Palestine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdelman2008154_146-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdelman2008154-146">[140]</a></sup> Eventually, the Arab Revolt convinced the Agency that a central intelligence service was required and this led to the formation of a counter-intelligence agency known as the _[Ran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ran_(Haganah_unit) "Ran (Haganah unit)")_ (headed by [Yehuda Arazi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yehuda_Arazi "Yehuda Arazi"), who also helped to smuggle rifles, machine guns and ammunition from Poland to Palestine) and thereafter in 1940 to the creation of [SHAI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shai_(Haganah_unit) "Shai (Haganah unit)"), the forerunner of [Mossad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mossad "Mossad").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriling200528_147-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriling200528-147">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaffin197980_148-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaffin197980-148">[142]</a></sup>

### British and Jewish co-operation

Further information: [Notrim](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Notrim "Notrim")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/PikiWiki_Israel_6918_Wingates_Special_Night.jpg/220px-PikiWiki_Israel_6918_Wingates_Special_Night.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:PikiWiki_Israel_6918_Wingates_Special_Night.jpg)

Men of Captain [Orde Wingate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orde_Wingate "Orde Wingate")'s [Special Night Squads](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_Night_Squads "Special Night Squads"), possibly in [Kfar Tavor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kfar_Tavor "Kfar Tavor").

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ghaffis_in_Nesher_2.jpg/220px-Ghaffis_in_Nesher_2.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ghaffis_in_Nesher_2.jpg)

_[Ghaffirs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Notrim "Notrim")_ watching a settlement in [Nesher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nesher "Nesher") near [Haifa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haifa "Haifa"). British authorities provided the guns and uniforms.

The _[Haganah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haganah "Haganah")_ ([Hebrew](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_language "Hebrew language") for "defence"), a Jewish paramilitary organisation, actively supported British efforts to suppress the uprising, which reached 10,000 Arab fighters at their peak during the summer and fall of 1938. Although the British administration did not officially recognise the _Haganah_, the British security forces cooperated with it by forming the [Jewish Settlement Police](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_Settlement_Police "Jewish Settlement Police"), [Jewish Supernumerary Police](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_Supernumerary_Police "Jewish Supernumerary Police"), and [Special Night Squads](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_Night_Squads "Special Night Squads"). The Special Night Squads engaged in activities described by colonial administrator [Sir Hugh Foot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hugh_Mackintosh_Foot,_Baron_Caradon "Hugh Mackintosh Foot, Baron Caradon"), as 'extreme and cruel' involving torture, whipping, abuse and execution of Arabs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a_1-26"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2009a-1">[1]</a></sup>

The British authorities maintained, financed and armed the Jewish police from this point onward until the end of the Mandate,<sup id="cite_ref-149"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-149">[143]</a></sup> and by the end of September 1939 around 20,000 Jewish policeman, supernumeraries and settlement guards had been authorised to carry arms by the government,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwedenburg2003220_3-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwedenburg2003220-3">[3]</a></sup> which also distributed weapons to outlying Jewish settlements,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999132_150-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999132-150">[144]</a></sup>and allowed the Haganah to acquire arms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECleveland2000255_151-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTECleveland2000255-151">[145]</a></sup> Independently of the British, _[Ta'as](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ta%27as "Ta'as")_, the Haganah's clandestine munitions industry, developed an 81-mm mortar and manufactured mines and grenades, 17,500 of the latter being produced for use during the revolt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999160_31-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999160-31">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESacharov200423_152-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESacharov200423-152">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdelman2008156_153-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdelman2008156-153">[147]</a></sup>

In June 1937, the British imposed the death penalty for unauthorised possession of weapons, ammunition, and explosives, but since many Jews had permission to carry weapons and store ammunition for defence this order was directed primarily against Palestinian Arabs and most of the 112 executed in [Acre Prison](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acre_Prison "Acre Prison") were hanged for illegal possession of arms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrämer2008292_154-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKr%C3%A4mer2008292-154">[148]</a></sup>

In principle all of the joint units functioned as part of the British administration, but in practice they were under the command of the Jewish Agency and "intended to form the backbone of a Jewish military force set up under British sponsorship in preparation for the inevitable clash with the Arabs."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999427_155-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999427-155">[149]</a></sup> The Agency and the Mandate authorities shared the costs of the new units equally.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999428_128-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999428-128">[122]</a></sup> The administration also provided security services to Jewish commercial concerns at cost.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999428_128-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999428-128">[122]</a></sup>

Jewish and British officials worked together to co-ordinate manhunts and collective actions against villages and also discussed the imposition of penalties and sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999427_155-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999427-155">[149]</a></sup> Overall, the Jewish Agency was successful in making "the point that the Zionist movement and the [British Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Empire "British Empire") were standing shoulder to shoulder against a common enemy, in a war in which they had common goals."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999426_156-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999426-156">[150]</a></sup>

The rebellion also inspired the Jewish Agency to expand the intelligence-gathering of its Political Department and especially of its Arab Division, with the focus changing from political to military intelligence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999149_157-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999149-157">[151]</a></sup> The Arab Division set up a network of Jewish controllers and Arab agents around the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999149_157-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999149-157">[151]</a></sup> Some of the intelligence gathered was shared with the British administration, the exchange of information sometimes being conducted by [Moshe Shertok](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moshe_Sharett "Moshe Sharett"), then head of the Jewish Agency, directly with the high commissioner himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999427_155-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999427-155">[149]</a></sup> Shertok also advised the administration on political affairs, on one occasion convincing the high commissioner not to arrest Professor [Joseph Klausner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Klausner "Joseph Klausner"), a [Revisionist Maximalist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revisionist_Maximalism "Revisionist Maximalism") activist who had played a key role in the [riots of 1929](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pro-Wailing_Wall_Committee "Pro-Wailing Wall Committee"), because of the likely negative consequences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999427_155-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999427-155">[149]</a></sup>

#### Forces of the Jewish settlement

> Table 1: Security forces and infrastructure created during the Arab revolt
> | Joint British-Yishuv | Independent Yishuv | Other Yishuv defence infrastructure   |
> | --- | --- | --- |
> | [Jewish Supernumerary Police](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_Supernumerary_Police "Jewish Supernumerary Police")  | [Mobile units](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobile_units_(Haganah_unit) "Mobile units (Haganah unit)") (mobile arm of the Haganah)   | [Ta'as](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ta%27as "Ta'as") <sup><b>†</b></sup> (weapons manufacture)  |
> | [Jewish Settlement Police](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_Settlement_Police "Jewish Settlement Police") | [Fosh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fosh_(Haganah_unit) "Fosh (Haganah unit)") (field companies) | [Rekhesh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rekhesh "Rekhesh") <sup><b>†</b></sup> (arms procurement)  |
> | [Mobile Guards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobile_Guards "Mobile Guards") (mobile arm of the Settlement Police)   | [Hish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hish_(Haganah_corps) "Hish (Haganah corps)") (field corps) | [Ran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ran_(Haganah_unit) "Ran (Haganah unit)") (counter intelligence)  |
> | [Special Night Squads](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_Night_Squads "Special Night Squads") | [Special Operations Squads](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peulot_Meyuhadot "Peulot Meyuhadot") | [Community ransom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Community_ransom "Community ransom") (defence tax)  |
> | [Tegart forts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tegart_fort "Tegart fort") and [Tegart's wall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tegart%27s_wall "Tegart's wall") | [Guards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guard_Corps_(Haganah) "Guard Corps (Haganah)") | [Tower and stockade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tower_and_stockade "Tower and stockade") settlement |

**†** Ta'as and Rekhesh were developed and expanded during the Arab Revolt but already existed before 1936 and the Haganah had been in operation from the earliest days of the Mandate.

#### Haganah intelligence services

There was no single body within the Jewish settlement capable of co-ordinating intelligence gathering before 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-oxford_158-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-oxford-158">[152]</a></sup> Until then there were four separate organisations without any regular or formal liaison.<sup id="cite_ref-oxford_158-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-oxford-158">[152]</a></sup> These were an underground militia, forerunner of the first official information service, _Sherut Yediot_ ([Shai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shai_(Haganah_unit) "Shai (Haganah unit)")); the _Arab Platoon_ of the [Palmach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palmach "Palmach"), which was staffed by Jews who were Arab-speaking and Arab-looking; _Rekhesh_, the arms procurement service, which had its own intelligence gathering capabilities, and likewise the _[Mossad LeAliyah Bet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mossad_LeAliyah_Bet "Mossad LeAliyah Bet")_, the illegal immigration service.<sup id="cite_ref-oxford_158-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-oxford-158">[152]</a></sup> In mid-1939 the effort to co-ordinate the activities of these groups was led by [Shaul Avigur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shaul_Avigur "Shaul Avigur") and [Moshe Shertok](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moshe_Sharett "Moshe Sharett").<sup id="cite_ref-oxford_158-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-oxford-158">[152]</a></sup>

### Role of the Revisionist Zionists

In 1931, a [Revisionist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revisionist_Zionism "Revisionist Zionism") underground splinter group broke off from Haganah, calling itself the _[Irgun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irgun "Irgun")_ organisation (or _Etzel_).<sup id="cite_ref-159"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-159">[153]</a></sup> The organisation took its orders from Revisionist leader [Ze'ev Jabotinsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ze%27ev_Jabotinsky "Ze'ev Jabotinsky") who was at odds with the dominant [Labour Zionist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mapai "Mapai") movement led by David Ben-Gurion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrämer2008242_160-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKr%C3%A4mer2008242-160">[154]</a></sup> The rift between the two Zionist movements further deteriorated in 1933 when two Revisionists were blamed for the murder of [Haim Arlosoroff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haim_Arlosoroff "Haim Arlosoroff"), who had negotiated the [Haavara Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haavara_Agreement "Haavara Agreement") between the Jewish Agency and [Nazi Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazi_Germany "Nazi Germany").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrämer2008242_160-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKr%C3%A4mer2008242-160">[154]</a></sup> The agreement brought 52,000 German Jews to Palestine between 1933 and 1939, and generated $30,000,000 for the then almost bankrupt Jewish Agency, but in addition to the difficulties with the Revisionists, who advocated a boycott of Germany, it caused the Yishuv to be isolated from the rest of world Jewry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBajohr2002122_161-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBajohr2002122-161">[155]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev199122,_29_162-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev199122,_29-162">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicosia200899_163-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicosia200899-163">[157]</a></sup>

Ultimately, however, the events of the Arab Revolt blurred the differences between the gradualist approach of Ben-Gurion and the Maximalist [Iron Wall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron_Wall_(essay) "Iron Wall (essay)") approach of Jabotinsky and turned militarist patriotism into a bipartisan philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBen-Ami200514_164-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBen-Ami200514-164">[158]</a></sup> Indeed, Ben-Gurion's own [Special Operations Squads](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peulot_Meyuhadot "Peulot Meyuhadot") conducted a punitive operation in the Arab village of [Lubya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lubya "Lubya") firing weapons into a room through a window killing two men and one woman and injuring three people, including two children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev1999386–387_165-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev1999386%E2%80%93387-165">[159]</a></sup>

From October 1937 the Irgun instituted a wave of bombings against Arab crowds and buses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147_166-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147-166">[160]</a></sup> For the first time in the conflict massive bombs were placed in crowded Arab public places, killing and maiming dozens.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147_166-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147-166">[160]</a></sup> These attacks substantially increased Arab casualties and sowed terror among the population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147_166-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147-166">[160]</a></sup> The first attack was on 11 November 1937, killing two Arabs at the bus depot near [Jaffa Street](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jaffa_Road "Jaffa Road") in Jerusalem and then on 14 November, a day later commemorated as the "Day of the Breaking of the _[Havlagah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Havlagah "Havlagah")_ (restraint)," Arabs were killed in simultaneous attacks around Palestine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147_166-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147-166">[160]</a></sup> More deadly attacks followed: on 6 July 1938 21 Arabs were killed and 52 wounded by a bomb in a Haifa market; on 25 July a second market bomb in Haifa killed at least 39 Arabs and injured 70; a bomb in Jaffa's vegetable market on 26 August killed 24 Arabs and wounded 39.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147_166-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147-166">[160]</a></sup> The attacks were condemned by the Jewish Agency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147_166-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999147-166">[160]</a></sup>

### Role of the "peace bands"

The "peace bands" (_fasa'il al-salam_) or "[Nashashibi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nashashibi "Nashashibi") units" were made up of disaffected Arab peasants recruited by the British administration and the Nashashibis in late 1938 to battle against Arab rebels during the revolt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrämer2008291_85-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKr%C3%A4mer2008291-85">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGettlemanSchaar2003181_167-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGettlemanSchaar2003181-167">[161]</a></sup> Despite their peasant origins the bands were representative mainly of the interests of landlords and rural notables.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGettlemanSchaar2003181_167-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGettlemanSchaar2003181-167">[161]</a></sup> Some peace bands also sprang up in the Nablus area, on [Mount Carmel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mount_Carmel "Mount Carmel") (a stronghold of the [Druze](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Druze "Druze") who largely opposed the rebellion after 1937), and around [Nazareth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazareth "Nazareth") without connection to the Nashashibi-[Husayni](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Husayni "Al-Husayni") power struggle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999153_168-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999153-168">[162]</a></sup>

From December 1937 the main opposition figures among the Arabs approached the Jewish Agency for funding and assistance,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999153–154_169-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999153%E2%80%93154-169">[163]</a></sup> motivated by the assassination campaign pursued by the rebels at the behest of the Husseini leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-171"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-171">[g]</a></sup> In October 1937, shortly after [Mohammad Amin al-Husayni](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammad_Amin_al-Husayni "Mohammad Amin al-Husayni"), the leader of the Arab Higher Committee, had fled from Palestine to escape British retribution, [Raghib al-Nashashibi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raghib_al-Nashashibi "Raghib al-Nashashibi") had written to Moshe Shertok stating his full willingness to co-operate with the Jewish Agency and to agree with whatever policy it proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2009125_109-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2009125-109">[103]</a></sup> From early in 1938 the Nashashibis received funding specifically to conduct anti-rebel operations, with Raghib al-Nashashibi himself receiving £5,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999154_172-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999154-172">[165]</a></sup> The British also supplied funding to the peace bands and sometimes directed their operations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999154_172-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999154-172">[165]</a></sup>

Fakhri Nashashibi was particularly successful in recruiting peace bands in the [Hebron hills](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mount_Hebron "Mount Hebron"), on one occasion in December 1938 gathering 3,000 villagers for a rally in [Yatta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yatta,_Hebron "Yatta, Hebron"), also attended by the British military commander of the Jerusalem District General [Richard O'Connor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_O%27Connor "Richard O'Connor").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999154_172-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999154-172">[165]</a></sup>

Just two months earlier, on 15 October 1938, rebels had seized the [Old City](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_City_(Jerusalem) "Old City (Jerusalem)") and barricaded the gates.<sup id="cite_ref-horne237_173-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-horne237-173">[166]</a></sup> O'Connor had planned the operation by which men of the [Coldstream Guards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coldstream_Guards "Coldstream Guards"), [Royal Northumberland Fusiliers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Northumberland_Fusiliers "Royal Northumberland Fusiliers") and [Black Watch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Watch "Black Watch") recaptured the Old City, killing 19 rebels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999154_172-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999154-172">[165]</a></sup> He was later to win fame as the field commander for [Operation Compass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Compass "Operation Compass") in World War II, in which his forces completely destroyed a much larger Italian army—a victory which nearly drove the [Axis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axis_Powers "Axis Powers") from Africa, and in turn, led [Adolf Hitler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adolf_Hitler "Adolf Hitler") to send the [German Africa Corps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deutsches_Afrika_Korps "Deutsches Afrika Korps") under [Erwin Rommel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erwin_Rommel "Erwin Rommel") to try to reverse the situation.

Towards the end of the revolt in May 1939 the authorities dissolved the peace bands and confiscated their arms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1999154_172-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1999154-172">[165]</a></sup> However, because members of the bands had become tainted in the eyes of the Palestinian Arabs, and some were under sentence of death, they had little choice but to continue the battle against the national movement's leadership, which they did with the continuing help of the Zionist movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2009198_174-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2009198-174">[167]</a></sup>

### Role of rebel leaders

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Palestinian_rebels_1937.jpg/220px-Palestinian_rebels_1937.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Palestinian_rebels_1937.jpg)

Rebels, some mounted on horses, posing with their rifles and a [Palestinian Arab flag](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestinian_flag "Palestinian flag") emblazoned with a cross and crescent, 1937

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Abd_al-Rahim_Hajj_Muhammad_portrait%2C_cropped.jpg/220px-Abd_al-Rahim_Hajj_Muhammad_portrait%2C_cropped.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Abd_al-Rahim_Hajj_Muhammad_portrait,_cropped.jpg)

[Abd al-Rahim al-Hajj Muhammad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abd_al-Rahim_al-Hajj_Muhammad "Abd al-Rahim al-Hajj Muhammad") was designated the "General Commander of the Revolt" by the [Central Committee of National Jihad in Palestine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Committee_of_National_Jihad_in_Palestine "Central Committee of National Jihad in Palestine")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Farhan_Sa%27di_arrested_by_British.jpg/220px-Farhan_Sa%27di_arrested_by_British.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Farhan_Sa%27di_arrested_by_British.jpg)

[Farhan al-Sa'di](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Farhan_al-Sa%27di "Farhan al-Sa'di") following his arrest by [British Mandatory police](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestine_Police "Palestine Police"), 1937. He was later executed. Al-Sa'di was a key actor in setting off the revolt with his April 1936 attack on a bus, which left two Jewish passengers dead.

At least 282 rebel leaders took part in the Arab Revolt, including four Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2009167_175-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2009167-175">[168]</a></sup> Rebel forces consisted of loosely organized bands known as _fasa'il_ <sup id="cite_ref-176"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-176">[169]</a></sup> (sing: _fasil_).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwedenburg2003125_177-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwedenburg2003125-177">[170]</a></sup> The leader of a _fasil_ was known as a _qa'id al-fasil_ (pl. _quwwa'id al-fasa'il_), which means "band commander".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwedenburg2003139_178-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwedenburg2003139-178">[171]</a></sup> The Jewish press often referred to them as "brigands", while the British authorities and media called them "bandits", "terrorists", "rebels" or "insurgents", but never "nationalists".<sup id="cite_ref-horne228_179-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-horne228-179">[172]</a></sup> _Ursabat_ (meaning "gangs") was another Arabic term used for the rebels,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnglim20059_180-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnglim20059-180">[173]</a></sup> and it spawned the British soldiers' nickname for all rebels, which was _Oozlebart_.<sup id="cite_ref-horne228_179-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-horne228-179">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnglim20059_180-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnglim20059-180">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-181">[174]</a></sup>

According to historian Simon Anglim, the rebel groups were divided into general categories: _mujahadeen_ and _fedayeen_. The former were guerrillas who engaged in armed confrontations, while the latter committed acts of sabotage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnglim20059_180-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnglim20059-180">[173]</a></sup> According to later accounts of some surviving rebel leaders from the Galilee, the _mujahideen_ maintained little coordination with the nominal hierarchy of the revolt. Most ambushes were the result of a local initiative undertaken by a _qa'id_ or a group of _quwwa'id_ from the same area.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwedenburg2003125_177-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwedenburg2003125-177">[170]</a></sup>

#### Galilee

Abdul Khallik was an effective peasant leader appointed by [Fawzi al-Qawuqji](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fawzi_al-Qawuqji "Fawzi al-Qawuqji") who caused great damage and loss of life in the [Nazareth District](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nazareth_District&action=edit&redlink=1 "Nazareth District (page does not exist)") and was thus a significant adversary of the Mandate and Jewish settlement authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_238_182-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_238-182">[175]</a></sup> He was trapped by British troops in a major engagement on 2 October 1938 and was killed whilst trying to lead his men to safety.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_238_182-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_238-182">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-183">[176]</a></sup> [Abu Ibrahim al-Kabir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abu_Ibrahim_al-Kabir "Abu Ibrahim al-Kabir") was the main Qassamite rebel leader in the [Upper Galilee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_Galilee "Upper Galilee") and was the only active rebel leader on the ground who was a member of the Damascus-based Central Committee of National Jihad.<sup id="cite_ref-KedourieQassamite_184-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-KedourieQassamite-184">[177]</a></sup> [Abdallah al-Asbah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdallah_al-Asbah "Abdallah al-Asbah") was a prominent commander active in the [Safad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Safad "Safad") region of northeastern Galilee. He was killed by British forces who besieged him and his comrades near the border with [Lebanon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebanon "Lebanon") in early 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-Ghandour_185-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-Ghandour-185">[178]</a></sup>

#### Jabal Nablus area

[Abd al-Rahim al-Hajj Muhammad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abd_al-Rahim_al-Hajj_Muhammad "Abd al-Rahim al-Hajj Muhammad") from the [Tulkarm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tulkarm "Tulkarm") area was a deeply religious, intellectual man and as a fervent anti-Zionist, he was deeply committed to the revolt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224–226,_239_186-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224%E2%80%93226,_239-186">[179]</a></sup> He was regarded second to Qawukji in terms of leadership ability and maintained his independence from the exiled rebel leadership in Damascus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224–226,_239_186-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224%E2%80%93226,_239-186">[179]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwedenburg200388_187-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwedenburg200388-187">[180]</a></sup> He personally led his _fasa'il_ and carried out nighttime attacks against British targets in the revolt's early stage in 1936. When the revolt was renewed in April 1937, he established a more organised command hierarchy consisting of four main brigades who operated in the north-central highlands (Tulkarm-Nablus-Jenin area). He competed for the position of General Commander of the Revolt with Aref Abdul Razzik, and the two served the post in rotation from September 1938 to February 1939, when al-Hajj Muhammad was confirmed as the sole General Commander.

Al-Hajj Muhammad refused to carry out political assassinations at the behest of political factions, including al-Husayni, once stating "I don't work for Husayniya ('Husanyni-ism'), but for wataniya ('nationalism')."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwedenburg200387_188-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwedenburg200387-188">[181]</a></sup> He is still known by Palestinians as a hero and martyr and is regarded as a [metonym](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metonym "Metonym") "for a national movement that was popular, honourable, religious, and lofty in its aims and actions."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwedenburg200330_189-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwedenburg200330-189">[182]</a></sup> He was shot dead in a firefight with British forces outside the village of [Sanur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanur,_Jenin "Sanur, Jenin") on 27 March 1939, after Farid Irsheid's peace band informed the authorities of his location.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224–226,_239_186-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224%E2%80%93226,_239-186">[179]</a></sup>

[Yusuf Abu Durra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yusuf_Abu_Durra "Yusuf Abu Durra"), a Qassamite leader in the [Jenin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jenin "Jenin") area, was born in [Silat al-Harithiya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silat_al-Harithiya "Silat al-Harithiya") and before becoming a rebel worked as a Gazoz vendor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_226,_228,_239–240_190-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_226,_228,_239%E2%80%93240-190">[183]</a></sup> He was said to be a narrow-minded man who thrived on extortion and cruelty and thus became greatly feared.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_226,_228,_239–240_190-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_226,_228,_239%E2%80%93240-190">[183]</a></sup> [Yusuf Hamdan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yusuf_Hamdan "Yusuf Hamdan") was Durra's more respected lieutenant and later a leader of his own unit; he was killed by an army patrol in 1939 and buried in [al-Lajjun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Lajjun "Al-Lajjun").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_226,_228,_239–240_190-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_226,_228,_239%E2%80%93240-190">[183]</a></sup> Durra himself was apprehended by the [Arab Legion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Legion "Arab Legion") in [Transjordan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emirate_of_Transjordan "Emirate of Transjordan") on 25 July 1939 and subsequently hanged.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_226,_228,_239–240_190-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_226,_228,_239%E2%80%93240-190">[183]</a></sup>

[Fakhri Abdul Hadi](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Fakhri_Abdul_Hadi&action=edit&redlink=1 "Fakhri Abdul Hadi (page does not exist)") of [Arrabah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arraba,_Jenin "Arraba, Jenin") worked closely with Fawzi al-Qawukji in 1936, but later defected to the British authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_238_182-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_238-182">[175]</a></sup> He bargained for a pardon by offering to collaborate with the British on countering rebel propaganda.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_238_182-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2003224,_238-182">[175]</a></sup> Once on the payroll of the British consul in Damascus, [Gilbert Mackereth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gilbert_Mackereth "Gilbert Mackereth"), he carried out many attacks against the rebels in 1938–1939 as leader of his own "peace band".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwedenburg2003121_191-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwedenburg2003121-191">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFryMacKerethRabinovich1985172_192-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFryMacKerethRabinovich1985172-192">[185]</a></sup>

> _Aref had a little mare  
> Its coat as white as snow  
> And where that mare and Aref went  
> We're jiggered if we know._ – British Army verse.
